1607960001|4.0|This edition of [[ASIN:1607960001 The Elements of Style]] looks like some new edition of the classic for wordsmiths and students, but it's not. The title sounds right, but this is merely one of many a reissues of the original title \\"The Elements of Style\\" written by Cornell University English professor William Strunk, Jr., who wrote it 1918. But this is not the version that is also known as \\"Strunk & White.\\" The product description is incorrect.<br /><br />If you're looking for the writing style guide that is in common use, [[ASIN:0205313426 The Elements of Style (4th Edition)]], by William Strunk, Jr. and E. B. White is the correct version, the one that Time Magazine listed as \\"one of the 100 best and most influential books written in English since 1923\\" in their August 30, 2011 issue.<br /><br />I made the mistake of ordering this paperback edition a few years ago when I was in a rush and ordered it along with another copy of author Stephen King's [[ASIN:0743455967 On Writing]], as both are what some of us consider essentials for writers. Simply put, I should have read the details more carefully... mea culpa.<br /><br />There are Kindle reissues of the 1918 edition, most notably [[ASIN:B0058I7TFI THE ELEMENTS OF STYLE (UPDATED 2011 EDITION) The All-Time Bestselling Book on Writing English Newly Edited (Special 2011 Edition) The Elements of Style OVER 10 MILLION COPIES SOLD! [Revised]]], which notes that it is newly revised and edited by Chris Hong, who is listed in the electronic flyleaf as a former editor at Harvard University, and bears a © 2011 The Elements of Style Press notation. This particular edition does have a slightly better layout on the Kindle, and the table of contents is well hyperlinked, but no mention is made of original author William Strunk, Jr., except for his name on the flyleaf.<br /><br />Among the other Kindle editions are [[ASIN:B003GSLX5Y The Elements of Style (Original Edition)]], with the quill on it's cover is basic, and it costs under a couple of dollars. But if you're going for basic, the [[ASIN:B005IT0V8O The Elements of Style]] edition with its generic cover is a no-cost freebie. And there are other Kindle editions if you search.<br /><br />The problem with these 1918 editions is that the writing style is dated. Original author Strunk had died in 1946, and in 1959, the publishers commissioned writer and author E. B. White, well known for his [[ASIN:0061124958 Charlotte's Web]], that classic for children, to revise the 41-year-old text. This was fitting as Strunk had been one of White's professors at Cornell. White's rework of the book was extremely well received, and further editions of the work followed in 1972, 1979, and 1999. White's expansion and modernization of Strunk's original became informally known as Strunk & White. As to whether it's considered a valid part of a writer's bookshelf, more than ten million copies of the three editions of Strunk & White have been sold.<br /><br />The history of this writing manual can be found in Mark Garvey's [[ASIN:B003STCKZ8 Stylized: A Slightly Obsessive History of Strunk & White's The Elements of Style]], which was published in 2009, an excellent book in its own right.<br /><br />So if you're looking for the real Strunk & White, [[ASIN:0205313426 The Elements of Style (4th Edition)]], by William Strunk, Jr. and E. B. White, is one you want, and it's only available in paper editions, except for the rare [[ASIN:0205632645 The Elements of Style: 50th Anniversary Edition]], which is now a collectable. Otherwise, you may enjoy one of the Kindle editions listed here, but think of them more as an interesting background than as contemporary writing style references.<br /><br />10/21/2012a couple of dollars. B008OJ291W|4.0|Only Jeff Lynne could accomplish something like this.  When I heard that Mr. Lynne was going to completely re-record the greatest hits of ELO from scratch, I was quite curious as to the outcome.  I'm a music aficionado, and I've heard my share of re-recordings; as you know - most of them are awful.  Most re-recordings are slapped together, pulse-less afterthoughts.  Not this time.  Jeff Lynne has managed not only to re-create the excitement and mystic wonder of Electric Light Orchestra, but to bring the additional thirty plus years of experience with him (with one exception that I'll get to shortly).  With these recordings, one feels the original sentimental joy of the song along with the thrill of 21st Century recording prowess.  The update in sound quality would be meaningless without the soul and passion that Jeff Lynne has woven throughout these fine selections.  Having said that, I must point out that the use of keyboard string sections is a bit of a bummer.  While this doesn't completely diminish my enjoyment of the songs, it does cause a slight cringe for a discerning ear that could never be fooled by synthesized violins, cellos, etc.  The fiddled-around-with-fiddles didn't make themselves apparent until \\"Sweet Talkin' Woman\\".  On most of the other tracks the fake strings are quite passable, after all, these are 21st Century keyboards.  However, on \\"Sweet Talkin' Woman\\", the strings sound like my 1985 Casio.  I'm sorry, Jeff, you should have, and could have used real strings.  It was a real low blow to your fans to use cheap sounding, fake string sections.  So, yes, I'm completely applauding the musicianship and authenticity of the re-creations, but I'm giving a thumbs down to the fake strings - hence the 4 star rating instead of 5.  I have to give at least 4 stars however, because real passion went into these performances along with an astonishing level of detail.  Don't get me wrong, this is an impressive effort worthy of many plays.  On \\"Strange Magic\\", for example, you'd never know the strings weren't real.  So, it's well worth the money.  Check it out!

0316223239|4.0|Five-year-old Jack's days are full of the usual types of activities for a child his his -- singing, drawing, playing with his toys, and of course, asking his mother nonstop questions about the world around them.<br /><br />But unlike other five-year-olds, Jack's world is limited to one room -- an eleven-foot storage shed where he has lived since birth. Unbeknownst to Jack, his mother was abducted for sexual purposes while a college student. Despite her own unimaginable depression, anger and despair, she has decided that her son will have the best possible childhood she can give him.<br /><br />And so, when Jack becomes older and begins asking more questions, his mother -- whose name is only given as \\"Ma\\" -- decides it's time for the two to escape. Their captor, dubbed \\"Old Nick\\" by Jack, only comes in the night, when Ma hides Jack in a wardrobe so he cannot witness what happens. Nonetheless, the effects of Old Nick's cruelty affect Jack profoundly, such as when he decides to punish Jack and Ma by cutting their electricity. Not only are they in the dark, but the tiny food supply quickly begins to spoil, leaving them desperate. Ma decides that they must escape or die trying; Jack, however, feels otherwise. He still cannot fully fathom the concept of a big world outside the confines of Room. Although he loves and has faith in his mother, he thinks it all sounds like a cartoon, a made-up drawing like his beloved Dora.<br /><br />Then, of course, the question becomes one of mental health, of adapting to normalcy when one's sense of what is \\"normal\\" has been so wildly altered for so long. How can a child like Jack be taught to play with other children, when until recently he believed he, Ma and Old Nick were the only people in the world? How can he be taught not to jump in fear at every little noise, every little gust of wind? Everyday life is full of countless things that most people never even think about, yet are almost insurmountable obstacles for someone who's neverexperienced them.<br /><br />Despite the horrifying aspects of this story, Donoghue's choice to have it told through the eyes of a five-year-old lessen the intensity almost palpably. Also, hearing Jack's POV means there's no sentimentality, no pity -- Jack simply tells it like it is. And thus, it's impossible not to root for him and Ma, desperately hoping for their rescue, both physically and emotionally.

And it will cost more too.<br /><br />  Disclaimer first - I've read every Jack Reacher book and like many, I hate waiting a year for the next one to come along.  I read this one on the first day of publication.<br /><br />My first major problem is the whole story begins with Reacher being picked up as a hitchhiker by two men and a woman.  Through a highly convoluted code between Reacher and the woman we learn that the car is hers and she has been hi-jacked.  This silly code transpires for what seems like half a book, and has no payoff, except to help fill the page requirements to call this a book.<br /><br /> How many times have we read of Reacher getting into a situation with two, three, a dozen or more men and come out as the last man standing?  Now comes this plot that works ONLY because Reacher can't think of a method to dispose of two men, neither of whom have displayed a weapon, and whom he even has isolated one-on-one a couple times.<br /><br />Reacher's ineffectiveness allow the hijackers to get away and he then feels responsible for her death. Because of this the whole story is flawed.<br /><br /> And I wish Mr. Child would stop playing God with likable characters.  If this character is no longer needed in the story find another way of saying good bye rather than just killing them for no reason, or don't write them is such a likable way to begin with. But it seems like the only way for Mr. Child to figure how to move Reacher forward to his next book is make us like the woman and then have her killed.  He's used this trick before and you can pretty much bank on the fact that if a lady is involved, she will soon be dead.  And in this case there was no reason to kill her other than to give Reacher an out.  The plot was not served one bit by her death.<br /><br /> And as likable and sympathetic a character that she was, Reacher obviously doesn't care.  He shows no grief or human emotion at her passing and sets off cool and calm for his next book, in which he will find another Lee Child invention who will also be dead in Jack Reacher's wake. Only because the author uses the death as a shorthand for getting Jack to move on.<br /><br /> I'm still waiting for the next Reacher book and looking forward to Lee Child writing like he wrote before he became famous and didn't have to work at it anymore.

0142000655|3.0|For about the first 200 pages, I was in the firm belief that this book would trump Grapes of Wrath. It is hard to review this book, because I would not want to turn someone away from reading it. Reading is more about the journey itself than what you get at the end most times. However, I really felt like he was going after too much with this book. It started off very bold and very focused, but after the half way mark and until the end there was so much unnecessary stuff that any other author not named John Steinbeck would never have gotten published. It's like he wanted to add a lot of autobiographical stuff in there, but he wanted to have his story about Adam and his family. When I put it down, I was left feeling that this could have been an amazing book at 300 pages. The theme was difficult to grasp, and this was not the case with Grapes and some of his other books. I am not sorry I read it. It has given me some things to think about. The language, as always, was beautifully written. 0439023513|2.0|There are thousands of reviews that have already been written about the Hunger Games series.  I enjoyed the first book quite a bit, the second one, not as much and actually struggled to get through the third book and don't plan to re-read any of them.  After all the hype and the movies coming out, I wanted to know what they were about.    Initially, the overall theme seemed good,  although on further thought it is pretty contrived.  It is hard to believe, that a city like Panem could maintain control of its districts for over seventy-five years with the barbarism shown in the Hunger Games.  Children in any society are among its most prized possessions and while people might tolerate pain, famine and other suffering from themselves personally, to see their children taken year after year to feed Panem's insatiable thirst for blood, without response is inconceivable.    The style of writing is frustrating as well.  There are so many fragments of sentences floating around, that meaning is sometimes lost or, difficult to parse.  I understand using this as a way of bringing an urgency to a tense situation, like in the Battle Arenas, but it just makes reading difficult in the middle.  I liked the first book enough that I dealt with it.  By the third book, it was old and just flat out irritating.    The main character, Katniss, is pretty devoid of motivation in this book. The first book, she is motivated by love of her sister, the second book, by a desire to protect Peeta, but in this book, there just isn't anything except for hatred of President Snow.  Which is something, but it doesn't really drive her to do anything until the very end of the book.  In point of fact, Katniss isn't really even that important to this book.  The author could have killed her off in the first chapter and then written a book called \\"Assault on Panem\\" that would have been more interesting.  Even Katniss's actions towards the end of the book don't seem to have any real impact on anything, exceptto kill of some of the characters around her.  The end is forseen from the beginning, but it sure is a slog to get there.    The love triangle really falls apart in this book.  I'm not quite sure why it was ever there in the first place, except that Katniss and Gale have hunting in common and hung out a lot together.  Otherwise, he isn't a very interesting character and there certainly isn't much sizzle going on in the first place.  As time goes by, it becomes pretty clear that he is very much an \\"ends justify the means\\" sort of person, who is willing to do what it takes to win the war, at whatever cost of human life.  He's just not much of a love-interest, though.    Which brings the fact that the one likeable character in the whole series, Peeta, is all but killed off for the majority of the book.  He just doesn't exist.  And without his humor, positive out look, and deep inner-good, all you're left with is an intense, somewhat unstable Katniss to wallow in her own fears and depression.    Perhaps the goal of the author was to convey that in war, there is no \\"good side,\\" that both sides are busy with their propaganda machines and are willing to waste lives, even of their own citizens, in order to achieve victory.  Certainly, Katniss is nothing more than a tool in this book of a different spin-machine.    I guess I would recommend reading this book, if you need to know how things turn out, but if you are OK with letting your imagination fill things in, you are probably better off letting it go.

But having been impressed by his manner in some interviews over the years, I approached this book in the hope of a decent insight into the man. And the first chapter- detailing the bizarre sexual perversions of his grandfather- was amazing, awkward, unflinching and brilliantly told. And then after that, it seems that the ghostwriter left Manson to his own devices, leaving a few notes for the structure of the remainder of the book. I found that Manson steered away from the type of introspection and self analysis that would have interested me, and instead started pointing fingers.<br /><br />Teachers, Religion, intact, all the usual authority figures get a serve. But it gets tiring quickly. He'll attack someone for their hypocrisy and I would think...'Well fair enough.' Then paragraphs later he would gloatingly recount some wrongdoing of his own, but with no effort at regret or even self awareness. Now to a jaded old codger like me, I can find my critiques of the modern world in more appetising fare, and better written and researched as well. If you are a young fan, then you'll possibly be impressed at Manson's vehement attacks on 'the man.' But if you've been through that phase of life already, then you'll find little of interest here.<br /><br />I feel that the book never really explores who Manson really is, it's just some more publicity, cocaine, goth chicks, knives, self harm blah blah blah, the usual rock n' roll  excesses are here in the usual detail. But that's not interesting. Marilyn Manson has always sold himself as an interesting product of modern society, yet he describes the most banal rock story that any fan of music bio's has already read...and written far more convincingly than this, I might add. You'll find more suitable reading from [[ASIN:1416511946 The Heroin Diaries: A Year in the Life of a Shattered Rock Star]] or better yet Anthony Keidis' story in [[ASIN:1401307450 Scar Tissue]]. And those artists never claimed to engage in any great social discourse, the way Manson has sold himself to the media and fans all these years. An image that isn't addressed much at all here, and it feels like that's because it is only an image, not a true passion.<br /><br />It isn't all bad, that first chapter was utterly brilliant, but it all ends up like some myspace page by the end, with lists of rumours cribbed from the Web, standard drawings of internal organs and things embellished with some suitably 'Goth' font and poetry. Oh god, the poetry. That almost loses one star for me, but that's really a matter of personal taste so I'll be generous and leave it at that.<br /><br />One for the fans only, perhaps.

This should have been saved or lessened with some clever writing and only pits the film drawing too many artificial comparisons with Alien or District 9 which hurts its potential. It really can't decide whether it wishes to be a campy sci-fi/action/western/horror/mystery or much more, yet still has good moments but with some elements that are interesting but then forgotten(that steamboat in the middle of the desert was cool and begs questions that are never resolved or played with later in the story). The gold mining plot has potential and draws some cool imagery and possibility for the finale that goes all out for action and thrills with some emotion.<br /><br />An interstellar conflict point is alluded to and gives for a decent twist with the Wilde character before the final act. But lets face it, we knew there was something with her when we saw her walk naked amongst shocked but not excited cowboys in the trailer. By this point you chuck away your eyes for originality and hope for a good finale, which the film provides without too much reach for a happy ending that I feared for Craig's character.<br /><br />Parting thought, the film was not the stinker many have said, but could have been better with less screenwriters and an extra script doctor or two. Steven Spielberg Executive-Produced with director Jon Favreau(oh yeah, look for an almost Hitchock-esque cameo early on)gives what he has, which if you like his films is usually enough, both for casual viewers and the obvious CGI nuts, though even they may feel cheated.<br /><br />Bottom line, it could have been worse under lesser hands, but better with what they had. Is that the attitude to have following a good romp? Oh well, a solid three and a half on a good day but with an extra half for drawing out the Script-doc in me, as I said, with some clever extras it didn't have to be CGI fest-which it really isn't save for some eye rollers- but that's my opinion. Enjoy.

0765316994|5.0|Redshirts by John Scalzi tells the story of the support crew onboard the Universal Union Capital Ship, Intrepid, and all the perils they face on a daily basis. I've been sitting on this review for a while, partly because I've not had much time to write but mostly because it has taken me a long time to come up with the right words for it. I'm a relative newcomer to Scalzi, having heard lots about him and his various sci-fi works but having never sat down and read anything of his until Redshirts. My first impression - if his other work is anywhere near as good as Redshirts then I can see myself devouring the rest of his bibliography in no time at all.<br /><br />The story follows Ensign Andrew Dahl, newly assigned as a junior scientist onboard the Intrepid, complete with red shirt. Almost immediately he notices something strange - the support crew are very good at hiding, the away missions have an obscene amount of fatalities, and the officers always seemed to survive the most horrific of injuries and are back up within days ready to face the next away mission. Dahl is determined to find out what is going wrong on this ship, before the next away mission becomes his last. This is a book that exploits the concept of the Redshirt, that guy on the away team in Star Trek that you knew was going to die because he was wearing a red shirt. It pokes fun at all those 70′s - 90′s sci-fi TV shows in a number of overt and subtle ways. Decks six through ten always suffer from explosive decompression during a fire fight, consoles on the bridge blow up in a shower of sparks every time the ship is hit by an energy weapon, you know, the little things that make sci-fi TV unique. Redshirts is a book that feels more like a tribute than a parody - I found the whole story heartwarming, and never condescending. There are a couple of problems with the story, the first being that the whole story feels like a running gag and by the end you are just looking for the next punchline. The otheris that the entire story feels like an in-joke, and I feel like anyone who hasn't ever watched those 70′s - 90′s sci-fi TV shows will be excluded from enjoying the majority of the story.<br /><br />Looking at the characters, it is very easy to dismiss them at first as being cardboard cut-outs. The nature of this story is to look at the Redshirt concept, so every main character plays a stereotypical Redshirt who is trying to avoid their likely fate of either dying on an away mission or on deck six. There isn't a great deal of depth or complexity to these characters, but this has been done deliberately, fits within the context of the story, and has been executed perfectly. For those who love their characters, it will mean the book is less than accessible for about the first 30%, but once you get to the first major plot twist it all makes sense with the characters taking on extra layers of complexity because of the bounds by which they personalities have been artificially confined. That said, despite these characters being perfect for the story Scalzi wanted to tell, they are just lacking, and to be honest I can't remember any of the Redshirt's names except for Ensign Dahl and Jenkins!<br /><br />The writing style for this whole story feels like a script, which again fits in with the context of the story. This can be jarring to read during the early chapters, especially during some of the rapid-fire dialogue that goes on between the characters. Almost every line of dialogue is followed by `said Dahl' or `said Jenkins' or `said...' for whichever character just spoke. As I said, just like a script / screenplay. I'm not sure if this is typical of Scalzi's writing, or if he just wrote it that way because it made sense to the story, but once you start to realize what is going on the narrative style almost becomes a character itself and I found myself laughing a few times at the way the scene was written, not just how funny the content of the scene was. After the completion of the main story, there are three `Coda's' that act almost like short stories set within the same world. Each one is written with a different point of view style - the first is written in first person, the second is written in second person, and the third (my favourite) is written in third person. All three Coda's have a completely different voice and style but they all work perfectly and help to bring this story to a fitting conclusion.<br /><br />Redshirts is a book where your mileage will vary based on how familiar you are with sci-fi. I also picked up a copy of the audio book (read by Wil Wheaton, and in my opinion the best way to consume this story) so my wife and I could listen together during a long trip, and we were both in stitches the whole way. But when I played it for a friend they were less than impressed, especially since they just didn't understand a lot of the set-up material or the punchlines. Redshirts gets a 9.0 from me, but I completely understand if you cant get into it and don't like it.

0307720950|5.0|This is a good book on an interesting topic.  I run cabinet shop in Toronto and have been prattling to my wife about the remaking of the industrial revolution for a few years now.  Anderson sums up many of these themes with lots of interesting stories in an easily readable style.<br /> I think there are a few things worth adding.  First while digital fabrication technology is amazing it is only as useful as the people using it.  A cnc router won't make you a good cabinet maker any more that a word processor will make you a good writer or a digital synthesizer will make you a good musician.  A synthesizer enables a good musician to become a whole orchestra almost instantly.  But a bad musician still sounds like a bad musician and a bad writer is just as annoying as ever to read. What these technologies do is allow the talented craftsman, musician, writer to be more productive than ever, and also lower the barriers to entry for the people with talent who are not part of the established social hierarchy.<br /> In my own shop I don't have my own cnc equipment. When I take on a project like a kitchen, I simply email lists of parts (doors, drawers, carvings) to fabricators not far from my shop and in some cases the parts come back to me the next morning.  My suppliers don't stock inventory, they fabricate the parts digitally and so they can produce whatever I want in whatever sizes I want.  This is the easy part of my job.  The hard part getting the clients to decide on what they want, and figuring out how to fit everything they want into the space they have on their budget.  To use a car analogy most clients want something like a \\"Hummer/Lamborghini/Porsche/Lexus/Rolls\\" for the price of a Focus. They often send me 3d cad drawings of their dream kitchen.  It is nearly always like those famous drawings by Escher.  At first glance they seem very geometrically precise, but they can't exist in 3 dimensional reality. Squaring this circle is always a challenge, and demands a combining the skills of an expert cabinet maker with those of a psychotherapist.  The second hard part of my job is fitting cabinets which are always made to be regular shapes into old real houses which are never square or level. Accomplishing this task demands the skills of an expert finish carpenter, tricks that I learned from my grandfather.<br /> In short to be a cabinet maker in the digital age you still need all the skills of a traditional cabinet maker.  However what digital technology and advances in new technology in general mean is that small shops can now compete with large factories in a way they couldn't 30 years ago.  I can now offer my clients anything that large factory kitchen manufacturers could in the past.  For example, 30 years ago complex cabinet door styles could only be made custom at great expense using traditional cabinet shop tools or economically in large batches at big factories.  Now I can order 1 door if I need it economically.  And, I can beat mass production companies hands down in terms of service and speed.<br />In many cases I can also compete with mass producers on cost. This is because I have lower transaction costs. One of the things that frightens small scale producers is the fact that labour costs of small scale production can't compete with mass production particularly if the goods can be produced in places like China.  People say \\"They make that thing in China for $5, how can I compete\\".  However, if the small scale producer sells locally they don't have to compete with the $5 labour cost in China; they only have to compete with the $50 or $100 retail cost in their local market. The goods that are produced in China have a long list of transaction costs associated with them: transportation, wholesaling, retailing, packaging, inventory, obsolescence, corporate expenses and profit, mass market advertising and promotion. All these costs mean that the widget that is produced for $ 5 needs to sell for $ 50 or $ 100 to make a profit.  This leaves lots of room for local artisans to make a living, as long as they keep their transaction costs down.<br /> Anderson points out the digital crowd is rediscovering actual reality.  I think he does not go far enough in this. People like actual reality.  One of the things little noted in the frenzy of the digital revolution is the success of the Home Depot retail model.  30 years ago building materials was a virtual business.  Materials were stored in warehouses to which customers both commercial and retail had no access. Most businesses would simply phone the supplier, say what they wanted and give an account number or use a visa and it would be delivered, much like ordering things online but over the phone. Even if you went to a lumber yard, you would usually go to a desk and order things and they would be brought out to you. Home Depot changed all this by putting everything on open shelves so people could go in a play with it.  The builders supply became playground for handy people. At the height of the virtual revolution, Home Depot took over the market for home building supplies by `going actual'.<br />  I find this in my own business.  While the web is a good way to get my name out, showing people real physical samples is the best way to close a sale.  After a visit I always make sure I leave a potential customer with a few samples to play with.  This way my brand sits on the kitchen table while they are trying to come to a decision.<br /> All this points to the possibility of a business model that Anderson hints at, but does not really explore; the return of the traditional neighborhood artisan. A few hundred years ago if you wanted a pair of shoes, or a coat or a piece of furniture you went to a shoemaker, or a tailor or a cabinet maker and told them what you wanted and they made it for you.  There was personal contact between the producer and the consumer, you could touch and feel the materials and say what you liked. People could take pride in their work and see the smiles on the faces of happy customers.<br />This was a world wiped out by mass production.  Huge production runs meant the artisan could not compete with mass produced goods.  But mass production brought its own costs.  The producer and the consumer became separated by a huge faceless corporate distribution system, which pretended to care, but most suspected really didn't. This was partially documented by Marx as worker alienation. The flipside, consumer alienation, is perhaps best documented by Monty Python.  Mass production also brings with it a whole host of transaction costs, noted above, which make it not as cheap as it might at first appear.<br />New production technology offers the possibility of changing all this.  When I go to a shoe store it is always a frustrating experience.  I always want some combination of style and size that they never seem to have in the back.  Imagine  however if a shoe store had say 50 or 100 basic shoes that you could try on for size and fit, as well as some other samples that you could use to pick the styles.  With the help of an expert shoemaker you could try on the fitting samples until you found something comfortable. Then you could use the style samples to mix and match all the colour and style details that fit your taste.  This shoe store would not have a big warehouse of boxes in the back but some rolls of material as well as some cnc cutting and printing machines and specialized assembly tools.  Depending on the complexity of the order you could go and have a coffee and then come back and pick up your order, or maybe come back the next day.  This shoe store would give you exactly what you want as well as have some real cost benefits.  There would be no packaging cost, low inventory costs, and much lower transportation costs.  (Compressed rolls of material are much cheaper to transport and store than packaged finished good). Many of these cost reductions would also be environmental benefits, such asless packaging and transport. And worker and consumer alienation would be a thing of the past.<br />This is how I run my cabinet shop and I think it has great potential. Sign shops already work on this model. Perhaps the mall of the future could look like the high street of old, with shoemakers, tailors and furniture makers crafting what you want when you want them.  The digital world provides the infrastructure and the tools, but the purchasing process would be actual and face to face.  The best of both worlds maybe?<br />(I also wrote a doctoral dissertation at Oxford which was in large part about the relationship of the world of things to the world of symbols, so I have also been interested in these problems from a philosophical perspective. My examiners, postmodernists who don't believe in outdated concepts like `reality', didn't take kindly to it.)

B0041045WE|4.0|Slayer's 2009 release World Painted Blood shows the band aging well. You'll find much of what you love about Slayer on the album: lightning fast riffing, screaming leads, blast beats... you get the drill. Its a solid release by a band that has a very strong, consistently heavy discography. WPB has some great highlights: Title track, Psychopathy Red, Hate Worldwide, Snuff, but also has some filler: Playing with dolls, Not of this god, a few more... WPB finds the band experimenting with new songwriting/performing techniques (for them) such as SINGING (Beauty Through Order) with overall good results and without straying too far from their winning thrash formula. As a longtime Slayer fan, I do put this one on and listen to it. It's a good record, but a bit unfocused sounding overall. It is missing some of the obvious occult themes that the band is famous for. Sure, we have some classic Slayer religious bashing (Not Of This god) but it is not particularly convincing. It is almost as if they put it on the album because they feel obligated...<br />WPB comes directly after 2006's Christ Illusion, an excellent album that links religion and war and shows much more consistency. When Araya yells \\"Ive made my choice... 666!\\" You fell the need to yell it with him... not so on World Painted Blood.<br />Christ Illusion is a much more focused and passionate release compared to World Painted Blood. Better songs make a better record.<br /><br />The vinyl pressing is beautifully done. Wonderful sonics, excellent printing and design that is carried through even to the blood soaked labels on the record. It is the first release in a while not to feature band photography. Comparing the original release with the version contained in the Vinyl Conflict box set is very minimal as they did such a good job with the initial release. The box set version has a litho wrapped cover while the sound has slightly higher levels. Very close in sound.<br /><br />Overall, World Painted Blood is a very goodSlayer album. Certainly not their best, but the band is aging well.

B00474GUEA|4.0|Someone has finally worked out the formula -- this album feels like it was designed to appeal to me on every level. I have always loved low-key, melancholy [[ASIN:B0000E6XJQ electronic]] [[ASIN:B000001HYW music]], particularly when used as a soundtrack for night drives. And here we have an album with that very concept as its title! If it had come out during my wasted youth full of techno pedantry, it would have been my favourite album of all time.<br /><br />It certainly comes with impeccable post-punk credentials. \\"Healer\\" sounds like a combination of [[ASIN:B001GQXRIO Joy Division's]] \\"Isolation\\" (the drums) and \\"Autosuggestion\\" (the opening intonation), and there's a [[ASIN:B004VPAXHU Kate Bush]] cover to complete the eighties connection. It's a phenomenal cover, too, with that [[ASIN:B00002DE4H New Order-ish]] melodic pulse and that ever so lonely synth-guitar lead. And the rueful airy female vocals sing about \\"you and me,\\" and \\"did you hear about the deal they're making, oh.\\" And the pulse drives steadily forward, like a lusher \\"[[ASIN:B000FWHVKA Silent Shout]].\\" It is bliss, I tell you.<br /><br />This is exactly what that early-2000s new-wave [[ASIN:B00006BTCA revival fad]] should have sounded like -- the weepy, puffy-eyed lonely verses echoing in empty space in \\"I Want Your Love,\\" then breaking into sexy keyboards and overdubbed cooing in the chorus. A slow start in \\"Mask\\" eventually steps up into a strutting chant perfect for the dancefloor, or perhaps for driving around at 2 AM and pretending to be heroic. Close to the end of the album, \\"Let's Make This A Moment To Remember\\" is an ultra-seductive instrumental full of minor-key synths and gentle piano melodies. The deliberately sparse production makes a lot of this music seem pretty simple on first listen, but when they relax the reins a little, a surprising amount of detail emerges -- in the span of a minute, there's quite a lot going on in \\"Let's Make This A Moment,\\" layers of silk and satin and sweet murmurs.<br /><br />The concept does gets stretched thin, though. The album closer \\"Tick Of The Clock\\" is basically fifteen minutes of nothing, the barest elements of a rhythm section that sound like bits of unfinished songs. \\"Tomorrow Is So Far Away\\" might have been better without the piercing keyboards, repetitive vocals, and two-minute-long howling-wind outro; the flute-like melody should have gone in a better song. Those are just two weak spots, but together they take up over twenty minutes, over a third of the album's running time. Replacing these tracks with some of the B-sides available on the deluxe edition would have made Night Drive sound a lot more focused.<br /><br />Plus, it goes without saying, but it's probably a good idea not to pay too much attention to the lyrics. The listless, narcoleptic delivery actually does a lot to sell these flowery expressions, like \\"when I came to this world / I arrived in a storm / alone in the rain / falling out from the clouds,\\" making them sound more like half-asleep thought fragments than self-absorbed monologue. But there's just not a lot there in lines like \\"I'll be a boy / I'll be a girl / see my reflection / it's just a reflection.\\" It's all about creating a mood of luxurious, film-star loneliness, but these are the meaningless thoughts one has in the cab while being ferried between clubs, and forgets the next morning. Not exactly the tortured dancefloor introspection of [[ASIN:B001ANGPSW Hercules And Love Affair]].<br /><br />This is basically a mood piece, but it's made with impressive artistry. I don't feel like I live in its world or have any connection to it, the way I did with [[ASIN:B000003RGL Dubnobasswithmyheadman]], but it is very pretty, and I do enjoy it very much. Who knows, maybe it'll be your favourite album.

0375842209|4.0|In the span of two weeks, I've read two novels set during WWII despite hating war books. I enjoyed both of them (Code Name Verity and The Book Thief) for their unique take on the genre. The Book Thief takes place in Germany as the war effort is revving up and spinning out of control.<br /><br />I appreciated Zusak's originality. Narrated by Death, The Book Thief is full of his quips and asides, which refreshes this oft recounted tale. Characters are also multi-faceted; there is no heroic German who takes in a pitiable but hopeful Jew. I'd argue that even Hitler, a man who history has been rightfully represented as 100% pure evil, manages to demonstrate complexity. In The Book Thief, actions have consequences and choices are painted with ambiguity. Zusak does not sugarcoat the truths of wartime but he does not oversell them to the point of pity porn either. Triumph and loss are intertwined because humans can never, especially in times of war, separate the good from the bad.<br /><br />I enjoyed Zusak's narrative choices as well. By choosing to narrate with Death, who benefits from near omnipresence and omniscience about the past, Zusak foreshadowed or in some cases outright exposed what was going to happen to characters. In normal circumstances, I'd expect the plot spoilers to mar my enjoyment of the novel, but in The Book Thief, it works extraordinarily well. We know the whats but not the whys, and discovering how things unfold leading up to Death's announcements is the whole fun of it.<br /><br />There is so much to analyze in this novel--the significance of colors, the facts of childhood in wartime, Zusak's style of prose itself--but what I'll remember best are two themes: the power of language and the ambiguity of humankind. Liesel, our eponymous \\"book thief\\", uses language to construct her world. Words can heal (they can make friendships; they can apologize) but they also can damage (they can start wars; they can denigrate an entire social identity). By findingthe power of language throughout the novel, Liesel becomes a moral individual. But not all of her choices, nor the choices of the many other characters, are unequivocally good. In Death's own words, he is \\"haunted by humans\\" because of their complexity of behavior, thought, and feeling. How can we be so capable of evil yet so capable of good? Countless authors have posed this question, but Zusak threads the answers (or non-answers) to this question into a broader historical and temporal fabric in a masterful way.

0812992784|4.0|Salman Rushdie is known for his fiction (Shame, The Moor's Last Sigh and others), but it was The Satanic Verses which forced him into hiding and police protection.  His memoir, Joseph Anton: A Memoir, tells the story of his life with particular focus on his experience following the publication of that book.  Frankly, I have never finished any of Rushdie's works of fiction and parts of this book I had to work through as well.  However, the effort was well worth it because of the insights he provides into why he was forced to go underground, the full defense of his book and its literary origins, and how he finally was able to get back a modicum of normalcy.  Readers also learn what it was like on a day-to-day basis to deal with living under full-time protection and what it meant to his family, career, and self-image.  The book has, for me, more detail and repetition than necessary, but the emotional effect was profound.  I began to identify with Rushdie and the frustrations he faced.  Rushdie uses third person to tell his story, but I'll not give away why the book is titled Joseph Anton: A Memoir.  If you have an interest in what happened to Rushdie, his take on why it happened, and who Rushdie became as a result - this book is for you.  A key learning? But it's also delivered masterfully; King's narrative isn't very dense, so the book is easy to digest. This, however, doesn't mean it is shallow, but rather accessible. Don't worry, you'll have plenty of detail to devour. Really.<br /><br />\\"It\\" is an internal odyssey of dramatic realization, friendship, love, passion, trauma and unnerving terror. Stephen King grabs all the important components of a good horror story, and stretches them to a fantastic lenght, while using, as the main antagonist, a creature evidently inspired by H. P. Lovecraft's cosmic horror, an apparently all-mighty, evil and eternal beast, who makes the lives of seven already disgraced children a living hell, hell he wants to extend for all eternity, as he disturbingly claims: no one really dies in Derry.<br /><br />The lenght wasn't an issue to me, because even on the first 50 pages you're in for an amazingly hooking tale. \\"It\\" is a rather light read I'd suggest to pretty much everyone over 13 years old. And soon enough, I was reading it again.<br /><br />If you're still doubting whether you should read this book because you already saw the series, let me tell you, the latter is roughly the 25% of the novel; and even that 25% dies short (and censored) to its equivalent to the book. B0074YVW1G|5.0|I have to admit, I almost stopped reading fairly early on in the book. It certainly left an impression on me, gave me plenty to think about, and has changed the way I see myself and the world. B0071XO8RA|5.0|I had to take a day or two to catch my breath after reading this novel (or collection, if you will) before I could write my review.  I became so attached to the characters that I feel almost in mourning.  I MISS Juliet; finishing the book left me feeling like a wonderful child of mine has left.  I've rarely read a story that works so well on so many levels.  The plot is entrancing science fiction.  Several themes wind themselves through the work, each engaging and thought provoking.  What do we owe to one another?  To what extent do means justify the end?  What is this species we call \\"human\\", really?  And of course the characterization is phenomenal.<br /><br />An interesting coincidence is that I read \\"Wool\\" immediately after re-reading  [[ASIN:B003XRELMA Make Room! Make Room!]] (the novel [[ASIN:B001QUM4IY Soylent Green]] is based on); and it felt like it could easily be a sequel (but not by the same author, since \\"Wool\\" is, in my opinion, better written).  Another work that seems thematically similar is  William Golding's [[ASIN:0399537422 Lord of the Flies]].  I won't venture to say Howey's book is better written, but his portrayal of human strength and weakness is, in my opinion, more realistic and based on real research instead of raw opinion.  I loved, for instance, that Howey worked a description of the Robber's Cave experiment into the work.  It's an important experiment that I'm familiar with from my scientific studies of human nature and it was wonderfully integrated without stepping outside of good story telling.<br /><br />One last thought:  \\"Wool\\" is incontrovertibly somewhat dystopian.  But the horror is not pervasive - there's lots of love and joy still in a world gone horribly wrong.  And neither are the solutions painted with sudden redemption, which only ever occurs in fiction.  As with all great fiction the setting is a means to an end and not an end in itself.  Bravo!

184195392X|5.0|I loved this book and I have now read it twice and undoubtedly will read it again...and again.  I consider it a great adventure story, a hugely emotional drama and, certainly on several levels, a life lesson.  I don't believe that Pi or Richard are stand-ins for something epic like God and Everyman, but I do believe that in the read you might get a glimpse of something outside the realm of Darwin. I have hesitated to write a review of this book as it is a bit like quicksilver...tricky to capture and imminently changeable, but here goes anyway.<br /><br />Briefly, and especially as many people here have already outlined the plot in detail, Pi is a boy with a serious interest in several spiritual paths.  The first part of the book follows his adventures in seeking out these theologies as well as his general growth and his family's circumstances, i.e., they own a zoo, they have decided to move to Canada for a more secure life and they have set sail on an ocean liner with their contingent of animals when a violent storm hits and the boat sinks.  Pi's family and all aboard are drowned.  Pi survives and in the second part of the book, ends up in a lifeboat with several animals, the most significant of which is a large, reasonably ferocious Bengal tiger named Richard Parker.  The tiger makes short work of the rest of the animals which leaves just Pi and the tiger facing each other at either ends of the boat.  I like to think that in addition to being a truly great and unique plot twist, this also represents adaption and hence survival because that is what these characters must do in order to live, and against all natural order they must do it together. Martel's writing is lyrical and magical and very emotional.  He paints a distinctive and vivid picture in the reader's mind and made it difficult, at least for me, to close the book and go to sleep.<br /><br />In a way I think the ending (which seems to have caused much controversy?) is really left up to the reader to writefor him/herself and I prefer to write it pretty much as Martel described it.  Did I see God?  Who's to say...would I know if I did?  Probably not. I do know that it's a smashingly good read and I highly recommend it.

B008K5T5AI|3.0|I spent the Summer re-reading all the Culture novels, so they're fresh in my mind for comparison. There is usually something that resonates long afterward; a character, a concept, a turn of plot, but why does The Hydrogen Sonata leave me empty?<br /><br />There doesn't seem to be a problem with narrative drive or pacing. The book zips along and there is plenty of action. It could be the characters. While the primary Mind Mistake Not... is the most fleshed out character, the humanoid protagonist is thinly drawn. There certainly isn't a scene stealer like Demeisen or Skaffen-Amtiskaw in this book. What was the point of the familiar other than a lost opportunity? No other Gzilt had a familiar. Then there is the question of motivation. The entire Gzilt society is behaving (appropriately) like a High School senior class in May, but what is the motivation for everyone else?<br /><br />The book feels quickly written and disconnected. At this point, if Banks writes a culture novel, it will translate to a certain amount of money. I'd hate to think this was his motivation, but it doesn't feel like it was a story he 'had' to get out of his system.<br /><br />The Hydrogen Sonata has all the elements of a Culture novel that I'm deeply interested in such as an important musical reference, plenty of chatter among Minds, and a setting that allows for the examination of deep philosophical questions. Does it matter? This question comes up multiple times. Arguably, it is the point of the book. It is also how I feel after finishing it.

B009AMANBA|4.0|Although \\"Lincoln\\" takes place over the space of its final four months, this is not a Civil War movie in that there are only two battlefield scenes. And as graphic and grisly as they are, both are rendered superfluous. The film, instead, focuses on the president's need to ram the 13th Amendment banning slavery through the recalcitrant House of Representatives before the Civil War comes to an end.<br /><br />As such, \\"Lincoln\\" is the antithesis of an \\"action\\" film which might explain why the audience I viewed it with contained few, if anyone, under the age of 40. Instead, its core power comes from scenes of people talking, discussing, and in many cases shouting. Mid-19th century America was a crude, raucous arena and the most exciting scenes take place on the floor of the House where members regularly heckle, browbeat and insult each other. In fact, the eloquent verbal savaging of one's political opponent is so lively that had C-SPAN existed then, it would be the most entertaining channel on television.<br /><br />One of the joys of \\"Lincoln\\" is that you are so immersed in the time period, that it is difficult to leave the theater afterwards without getting disoriented. You can feel the mustiness of the furnishings, the chill in the meagerly heated White House, and the constrictions of the clothing. Politics is a game so corrupt that the bribing and manipulating of politicians is openly accepted and Honest Abe is not averse to playing it to get the votes needed to pass the amendment. Ever the lawyer, he knows that the Emancipation Proclamation is not legally binding and that the eventually reunited Southern states will not heed it. Hence, the war can end with a Union victory with slavery still a fact of life below the Mason-Dixon Line.<br /><br />The passing of this landmark addition to the Constitution is always in doubt. The racism of the era is unflinchingly demonstrated, one of the core scenes being when fire-breathing abolitionist Thaddeus Stevens (an acerbically delightful Tommy Lee Jones) cannot avow on the House floor that all men are indeed, \\"created equal\\", knowing that such a radical declaration will alienate the swing votes.  The idea that black Americans (and all women might) someday get the vote is met with outcries of rage and disbelief.  It is a deal with the Devil that Stevens makes and he knows it.<br /><br />Much has been made of the acting of Daniel Day-Lewis in the title role and it is indeed a tour-de-force. Midway through, you forget it is an actor you are seeing instead of the actual personage. His Lincoln is sensitive, melancholy, profane, at times riled and always funny as hell, ready at the drop of the hat with a story or tall tale, most laced with R-rated language. Gangly and awkward, were he to run for office today, he wouldn't survive the first primary. Give Day-Lewis the Best Actor Oscar now and save us the ceremony.<br /><br />But I would give a Best Supporting Actress nod to Sally Field who fought for her role as Mary Lincoln. This sad woman, plagued by depression and migraine headaches, devastated by the loss of her son, Willy, is a study in iron tragedy. She both loves and hates her husband for his exalted occupation and his aloofness and the scenes where they argue over their children, both living and dead, have a poignant immediacy. Field inhabits Mary as much as her counterpart inhabits her husband.<br /><br />While not as great as I had anticipated, I woke up in the middle of the night thinking about it and relishing the characters and scenes from one of the most momentous epics of American history. Spielberg has always had a hit (\\"Saving Private Ryan\\", \\"Schindler's List\\") or miss (\\"Amistad\\", \\"1941\\") relationship with history but this latest offering is truly in the first category.y delightful Tommy Lee Jones) cannot avow on the House floor that all men are indeed, \\"created equal\\", knowing that such a radical declaration will alienate the swing votes. B005LAIGOQ|4.0|The Harry Potter franchise has left such lasting impact that, throughout the viewing of THE WOMAN IN BLACK, I half-expected Ron Weasley and Hermione Granger to apparate and lend a hand to their beleaguered old mate. A Hammer Films joint, THE WOMAN IN BLACK makes for elegant British gothic horror. It tells a dark tale of numbing loss and unholy vengeance. It's Daniel Radcliffe's first venture into cinema, post-Potter. And yet he's still dabbling in the supernatural.<br /><br />Susan Hill's eerie 1983 bestseller had already been adapted as a television mini-series in the 1980s and as a stage play, but the rejuvenated Hammer Film Productions thumbed it up for a go on the silver screen anyway. Daniel Radcliffe, all of 21 years old during the filming, plays widower Arthur Kipps who after four years still grieves for his wife's passing. His inertness has adversely affected his career at a London law firm to the extent that he's a hair's breadth away from getting sacked.<br /><br />Arthur Kipps gets one last chance to make good. He's dispatched to northeast England, to a remote village in which outskirts looms the creaky Eel Marsh House. In this ruined mansion, ringed by treacherous marshland and accessible only by a causeway, Arthur is to sort out a deceased woman's estate. But since this isn't an episode of SUITS, the spooky very soon emerges.<br /><br />A curse holds the nearby village in thrall, or not a curse but rather a vindictive spirit. Arthur's arrival is met with instant suspicion from the inhospitable townspeople, most of whom fear what Arthur's meddling may further stir up. Faced with brusqueness at every turn, Arthur Kipps presses on. And while he's so emotionally closed off that he rarely exhibits a reaction to the alarming stuff going on around him, the film does a very good job of jangling our nerves. The scares are of the make-you-jump variety, cheap but very effective and more restrained than garish. THE WOMAN IN BLACK does well in establishing its ghost story ground rules. We find out that the menacing figure that fleetingly graces your peripheral vision - a veiled woman clad in black mourning garb - is targeting children, is forcing them to engage in gruesome self-extermination. So, right there, a measure of tension is lifted. We know Radcliffe's character is moderately safe. And there isn't a child prominently featured in the film for whom we have a rooting investment. Arthur's young boy Joseph (played by 4-year-old Misha Handley, Radcliffe's godson in real life) is barely in it.<br /><br />Set in the Edwardian era (thus placing this story somewhen between 1901 - 1910), the film's biggest ace is the marvelously ominous set design of the Eel Marsh House. It's one of the spookiest haunted houses seen onscreen in recent years. As ever, in the auspices of a ghost story, children's toys very much become these creepy artifacts. I thought Radcliffe does okay. He plays a repressed widower and you may wonder why he doesn't panic more or just plain clear out when that empty rocking chair starts rocking or disembodied footsteps are heard or that distant window pane reveals a spectral woman gazing out. I think Arthur Kipps sees these unsettling events in another way, sees them as evidence of an afterlife in which he can reunite with his dead wife. One gets the sense that he deliberately seeks out the unrestful spirit. It's up to you whether this undertone exists and if it's communicated by Radcliffe's rather reserved performance. Also, with high tide closing off the causeway, where was he gonna go?<br /><br />THE WOMAN IN BLACK is rich in atmosphere and broodingly gothic. It's a restrained but effective chiller. Certain moments made me clutch the armrest. Perhaps one knock is that while it does maintain a thread of steady suspense, I think it fails in delivering that big payoff. It falls short of that epic grand guignol. It's touted as the most successful British horror picture in two decades, and so were you at all startled when Hammer Films announced a sequel, the story set to take place during World War II?<br /><br />The DVD's bonus stuff:<br /><br />- Audio commentary with director James Watkins and screenwriter Jane Goldman<br />- \\"Inside the Perfect Thriller: Making THE WOMAN IN BLACK\\" (00:09:32 minutes)<br />- \\"No Fear: Daniel Radcliffe as Arthur Kipps\\" (00:04:05 minutes)d when Hammer Films announced a sequel, the story set to take place during World War II?<br /><br />The DVD's bonus stuff:<br /><br />- Audio commentary with director James Watkins and screenwriter Jane Goldman<br />- \\"Inside the Perfect Thriller: Making THE WOMAN IN BLACK\\" (00:09:32 minutes)<br />- \\"No Fear: Daniel Radcliffe as Arthur Kipps\\" (00:04:05 minutes)

B000002GK1|5.0|Though 1994's \\"Superunknown\\" is generally looked upon as Soundgarden's apex, 1991's \\"Badmotorfinger\\" is the album that put Soundgarden on every rock fan's radar, and is arguably their \\"purest\\" album.<br /><br />Production:<br />Like their previous releases, Badmotorfinger (BMF) is full of grinding guitars, wailing vocals, and edgy rhythmic chaos. However, unlike their early records, BMF refuses to drift off into aimless murky garage-metal (like in \\"Ultramega OK\\") or delve into raunchy, ugly subject matter (check out \\"Louder than Love\\"). While those two records definitely had their share of fantastic material, BMF's consistency and overall musical quality leap galaxies ahead of its two predecessors. This is thanks, in no small part, to its razor-sharp production, which manages to keep the guitars clanking and grinding like chainsaws, and the amazing vocals WAYY up front. By 1991, Cornell had finally found his \\"sweet spot\\" as far as his singing capabilities go. He had found a balance between hitting high notes and giving his singing patterns the hooks they needed. Kim Thayil's monstrous guitars are right where they should be, and the rhythm section of the band is mixed in appropriately to keep a firm, rock-solid pace to each and every performance.<br /><br />Material:<br />As stated before, BMF finds Soundgarden at a very balanced stage in their career. The songs are still riff-driven, but the riffs contain hooks that far \\"outshine\\" the (occasionally) overblown Sabbath-esque riffage of their early career. \\"Rusty Cage\\" is arguably the best album opener of its kind, with a squalling-yet-distant opening riff that evolves into a quirky, fast-paced metal gem. \\"Outshined\\" contains possibly the most catchy metal riff EVER outside of the standard 4/4 timing. But in true Soundgarden fashion, the song doesn't feel off-beat or unnatural at all. \\"Slaves and Bulldozers\\" starts with a slow bass riff and a chiming guitar noise before becoming a familiar-yet-inspired rock music beast. \\"Jesus Christ Pose\\" is absolutely mindblowing, and from start to finish will have your head spinning like the helicopter blade-like beat that fuels it. \\"Face Pollution\\" is punk-rock inspired, and is mostly in average territory, but it's still fun to listen to. \\"Somewhere\\" is where Soundgarden starts to experiment with both psychedelia and alternate tunings, thanks to the Eastern-influenced songwriting of new bassist Ben Shepherd, who introduced some truly vital elements to the band. \\"Searching With My Good Eye Closed\\" is a foreshadowing of the dark, unsettling atmosphere that would dominate their next album, and it's a very intriguing listen. \\"Room a Thousand Years Wide\\" is a Thayil-dominated rocker that will have old-school metal fans banging their heads until it ends. \\"Mind Riot\\" slows the tempo down and reminds me almost of a pop-punk song, had it not been for the atonal-yet-melodic guitars, and it's one of their most underrated numbers. \\"Drawing Flies\\" is a balls-out bluesfest, this time written by drummer Matt Cameron. \\"Holy Water\\" is very disconcerting and discordant, and its subject matter only makes it more disturbing. That said, it's melody will get under your skin with repeated listens. \\"New Damage\\" is possibly the most abrasive song on the album (besides \\"Jesus Christ Pose\\") and I didn't particularly care for it, although it's not a bad song per se.<br /><br />Packaging:<br />The album cover itself is one of the most memorable I've seen, with a bizarre cyclone-like symbol superimposed over what looks like a shot of an audience at one of their shows. The font used for the band's name is my personal favorite, and I even drew an identical logo on my backpack in high school with white-out. The booklet contains all of the lyrics from every song, although you have to open the entire sleeve because the lyrics are written entirely horizontally. The band shot is one of my favorites, with Chris donning a bad-ass \\"Soundgarden\\" leather jacket, and Matt striking a \\"I'm too sexy for my tight pants\\" pose. It's the perfect representation of a band who's finally hit their confident stride.<br /><br />Conclusion:<br />This was the first Soundgarden album I've owned, and I don't regret it one bit. Although it's not my favorite Soundgarden album, I do believe it is the most representative of their fundamental sound. In a year where more popular bands from Seattle were changing the world with albums dominated by anthems (Nirvana's \\"Nevermind\\" and Pearl Jam's \\"Ten\\"), Soundgarden took a decidedly different route and released this noisy, balls-to-the-wall gem. Although they didn't achieve worldwide recognition until 1994, BMF still stands as perhaps every metalhead's favorite grunge album, and is arguably the heaviest album to come out of Seattle. Highly recommended.<br /><br />Standout Tracks:<br />\\"Rusty Cage\\"<br />\\"Outshined\\"<br />\\"Jesus Christ Pose\\"<br />\\"Searching With My Good Eye Closed\\"<br />\\"Mind Riot\\"<br /><br />Final Score: 9.2/10<br /><br />Fun Fact: This album landed a spot in Guitar World's Greatest Guitar Albums of All Time, at #45 (beating Metallica's \\"Black Album\\"). :D

B00913P6G0|5.0|Kendrick Lamar made a dent in the Hip Hop world with his mixtape Overly Dedicated back in 2010. In 2011 came the release of his highly acclaimed album Section.80 featuring immaculate production, great conceptual stories and high caliber lyricism from Kendrick Lamar. What makes Kendrick so appealing to me is the fact that he is from Compton, California, a place where a typical rapper would glorify the lifestyles of the hood such as gangs, drug dealers, being tough, etc. however Kendrick is quite the opposite of your stereotypical rapper from the CPT; he covers multiple topics of the hood from different angles from male and female perspectives as well as in first and third person view. Kendrick is quite the lyrical tyrant, and at the age of 25, his subject matter is very mature for his age. In an age where Hip Hop is being saturated with rappers such as the horrible French Montana, Lil B and Lil Wayne clones, Kendrick Lamar as well as his Top Dawg Entertainment family is slowly helping resurge lyrical Hip Hop with great content that kids could benefit from hearing in the near future.<br /><br />As for the album, \\"good kid, m.A.A.d city\\" is the major label debut album of Kendrick under the multi-platinum juggernaut Aftermath Entertainment, a label created by Dr. Dre whom has helped spawned successful careers for artists such as Eminem, 50 Cent and another Compton Rapper The Game with \\"The Documentary\\". Aftermath Entertainment has had a history in the past of signing artists, but never generating any solid album releases which include Raekwon, Rakim & Bishop Lamont; but luckily for Kendrick, Dr. Dre has faith in the fellow Compton MC to the point of releasing albums for him as soon as possible, which is rarely seen from the good doctor with the exception of Eminem or 50 Cent. This album is a concept album covering certain aspects of Kendrick's childhood living in Compton as well as his affiliations with his ghetto peers and family which is clearly heard  throughout. This album serves as reflective journal of Lamar's experiences growing up whether it be about drugs, women, thug life or just self-insecurities and aspirations, this is a very cohesive project that will surely expose Kendrick to a broader audience as well as give his loyal fans some new material to vibe to.<br /><br />Track By Track Review<br />1) Sherane a.k.a Master Splinter's Daughter - A great introduction track that serves as the opening sequence to the story. The story follows a young Kendrick falling for a girl named Sherane and all adult acts that follow. The character of Sherane pops out throughout the entire album either a person based on a real-life girl of Kendrick's or as a metaphor for the hood of Compton being the temptress trying to pull Kendrick into the darkside. As the story continues Kendrick steals the van and gets ambushed by some hoodlums in black hoodies. His parents than gives him a call and ask where he is at. As a song, its not great but as an intro to the album it works. 3/5<br /><br />2) B**** Don't Kill My Vibe - this song reminded me why I didn't like Kendrick from first listen back then, his voice can be awkwardly strange at times, and the hook for this song exemplifies that. But now that I have listened to Kendrick for almost 1 year and a half now, I've learn to listen past it. The song is nice and catchy with beautiful strings in production, Any Hip Hop song that takes time to incorporate real instruments into a song is always commendable. In this song Kendrick is basically reflecting his inner thoughts about his past and new found fame. 5/5<br /><br />3) Backseat Freestyle - Kendrick just going off bars for bars on the GOOD Music Producer Hit-Boy track. The only flaw I find with this song is that I find it more fitting on a mixtape rather than on an album. However, if you listen to the skits before and after, than the context of this song fits perfectly. But as a stand alone song, the production by Hit-Boy wasn't mindblowing to me butat least it wasn't complacent the whole way through. Kendrick Lamar was on his ignorant ish and even had his 2Pac moments haha.  3.5/5<br /><br />4) The Art of Peer Pressure - Great track, a story of a young boy riding around with the big boys getting caught up in grown man business. The story of how innocence can easily be lost when influenced by the wrong group of people, or in this case \\"the homies\\". Although the title does indicate the act of a person being pressured, the lyrics tell otherwise. As the story goes on, you realize that the character isn't actually being pressured at all, he just acts and adapts to the lifestyle he is surrounded by, but the interesting thing is that he never resists, he deems it as normal activity just because he grew up in that environment. Kendrick is always in top form when he is telling a story and this song is no different, the dark and eerie production adds great dramatic effect to the song as well. 5/5<br /><br />5) Money Trees feat. Jay Rock- If you are a Kendrick fan prior to this album than this song will feel right at home with you. This song kind of takes me back to his Overly Dedicated days with the mellow beat as well as a funny adolescent hook that Kendrick likes to do. It's nice to finally have his fellow Black Hippy partner Jay Rock on a Kendrick Lamar album because he was M.I.A on Overly Dedicated and Section.80, which was understandable but also felt incomplete (to me anyways).  I love the song for what it is; it's one of my personal favorites that I love to bump in the car. Ya Bisshhhh. 5/5<br /><br />6) Poetic Justice feat. Drake - Kendrick is known for his smooth and rapid flow, this song is a perfect example of that simple fact. Has a very sexy Janet Jackson sample roaming in the back ground, this song caters to the ladies in a sensual matter. It's rare these days to have a love song from a rapper that doesn't contains lyrics that refer to their male genitalia all the time, but like the title implies, this song is like a love letter to the females in a poetic manner. I prefer this Drake over braggadocios Drake any day..If there's one topic Drake can be consistent with, its women. The only complaint from me was that the hook could've been executed better, I would've actually preferred for Drake to try to come up for a hook for this song. This is an obvious next single so expect to hear this song a lot in the near future.  4/5<br /><br />7) good kid - Continuing from the last songs skit, here is where the story comes back into narrative and the concept of the album really comes to fruition. Produced by the legendary Pharrell, you just can't go wrong with that featuring a very eerie clarinet. Great lyricism from Kendrick and even Pharrell even contributes on the songs hook.  4/5<br /><br />8) m.A.A.d city feat. MC Eiht - The song for the Hoodlums. This song is maniacal with so many different things going on. Schoolboy Q's signature \\"YAWK YAWK\\" is hilariously incorporated within the song. Kendrick documenting the things he has seen and experienced in the mad city of Compton filled with bloods and crips running around, seeking vengeance, and even Kendrick's experience with laced marijuana which is what actually drove him to sobriety (true story). Mid-Song the song shifts into a different monster; the legendary MC Eiht makes an appearance and drops a little knowledge of the OG lifestyle. The 2nd half has also has 90's westcoast production with synthesizers and talk-box vocals. I loved the track, one of the best on the album. 5/5<br /><br />9) Swimming Pools (Drank) - The single that has taken the country by storm. Great production by T-Minus, great verses from Kendrick albeit a little short and an incredibly catchy hook. The album version contains an extra verse which makes the song that much better for me personally, and the skit at the end ties in with the album concept. One of my favorites of the album despite it being a little overplayed. 5/5<br /><br />10) Sing About Me/I'm Dying of Thirst - Running for 12 minutes, this is the longest track on the album as well as one of the most complex. These are 2 songs merged into one, but if you're listening to the album in its entirety than I doubt that you would even notice. \\"Sing About Me\\" tells the stories of 2 different people. It's hard sometimes to really pinpoint the perspective of the story because each verse feels like it is about someone different, 1st verse tells about a character that got shot from the previous song's skit. The 2nd Verse was a bit of a surprise because it connects with his previous album Section.80, and apparently whoever this character is, is the sister of Keisha.  The song is very bi-polar as well, once it switches up it goes from good to sad which is what I'm pretty sure what Kendrick intended. \\"I'm Dying of Thirst\\" contains a mix message that I still can't decipher, but the theme of this portion of the song is death and rebirth. I love songs that make me want to listen over and over and try to figure out what the story is. 5/5<br /><br />11) Real feat. Anna Wise - First I wanna say that the person who produced this song, Terrace Martin, is probably one of my favorite producers as of now. Fans know that Terrace produced \\"Ab-Soul's Outro\\" on Section.80, which was a masterfully crafted song that was essentially a jazz instrumental, and my personal favorite song on Section.80. This song feels like a dream, the light production with light clashes with the cymbals in the production. The only complaint I have with this song is the overly simplistic hook by Anna Wise, it felt lazy and uninspiring just reiterating \\"I'm real, I'm really really real\\". It was an extremely boring hook man and I just can't seem to force myself to like it. The end of the song also features an emotional skit of Kendrick's mom, just speaking to him. There is just something emotional of the words that the mother was speaking, it's a word of encouragement for Kendrick to become a man and leave behind the world he has grown accustomed to but never forget it and just become a role model for the kids tryna make it out of the same situation. It reminds me of the little monologue from (Bruce Wayne in Batman Begins) \\"A man is just flesh and blood and can be ignored or destroyed. But as a symbol... as a symbol, I can be incorruptible, everlasting\\". 4/5<br /><br />12) Compton feat. Dr. Dre - The last track of the album features the Godfather of everything Compton, the legendary Dr. Dre. As the closing track, Kendrick and Dre go back and forth to celebrate the city of Compton. I believe that this song ended the album perfectly, however I find the song to feel a bit generic at times. Not taking anything away from the song, because Just Blaze did an incredible job on the production. 3/5<br /><br />After listening to the album multiple times, I have to say that Kendrick created an amazing album thats consistent, diverse and substantial. The mini interludes mixed between songs really enhance the story Kendrick is trying to tell and each and every song just flows seamlessly.  For me personally, I will still rank Section.80 slightly above this album, mainly because I felt that Section.80 was more ambitious as far as production and storylines. But I still love this album, this is a great debut album for (the still growing) Kendrick Lamar, while I may not say this is a classic album just yet like I'm sure many Kendrick Lamar fans may try to imply, I will say that this is easily the best mainstream hip hop album of 2012. #HiiiPoWerng is like a love letter to the females in a poetic manner. I prefer this Drake over braggadocios Drake any day..If there's one topic Drake can be consistent with, its women.
